Evidence gaps
- **Data Provenance and Copyright**: There is limited transparency regarding the exact datasets
  used to train frontier models, complicating efforts to enforce copyright law, protect personal data,
  and track the origins of training errors or biases.
- **Scaling Limits**: It remains unclear whether current approaches – scaling model size, training data,
  and inference compute – will continue to yield predictable capability gains, or whether fundamental
  technical bottlenecks will soon cause progress to plateau.
- **Synthetic Data Reliability**: The long-term effects of training AI models on synthetic data generated
  by other AI models (e.g., potential ‘model collapse’ or amplification of bias) are not yet fully understood.
International AI Safety Report 2026 23

Current capabilities
Section 1.2
Current capabilities
Key information
— General-purpose AI capabilities have continued to improve across domains
such as mathematics, software engineering, and scientific reasoning, driven by
inference-time scaling and improved post-training techniques.
— Leading systems achieve expert-level performance on many standardised
evaluations, including professional exams and competitive benchmarks.
However, performance remains ‘jagged’: systems can easily solve complex problems
while failing at seemingly simple tasks (such as basic arithmetic, spatial
reasoning, or counting objects in an image).
— The ‘evaluation gap’ remains a major challenge: high performance on pre-deployment
benchmarks often overstates a system’s practical reliability and utility in complex,
unstructured real-world environments.
— AI adoption has grown rapidly, reaching hundreds of millions of users globally,
though adoption rates remain highly uneven across countries and regions.
Current capabilities across domains
General-purpose AI systems can now perform a
wide variety of tasks across multiple domains.
This subsection highlights their performance in
several key areas, drawing on evaluations and
empirical studies published up to December 2025.
Mathematics and logic
Frontier AI systems have made notable
progress in mathematics and logic. Using
inference-time scaling, leading reasoning
systems have achieved performance comparable
to human gold medallists on International
Mathematical Olympiad (IMO) competition
problems under test conditions (75, 76).
They can solve complex algebra, calculus, and
number-theory problems that previously required
specialised mathematical software or human
experts. However, these successes are often
achieved in structured settings with clear rules
and verifiable answers. When applied to open-ended
mathematical research or ambiguous problem
formulations, their performance drops significantly.
Software engineering
In software engineering, AI systems can write,
debug, and refactor code across multiple
programming languages. AI agents can now
reliably complete multi-step software engineering
tasks that take human programmers about half an
hour, up from under 10 minutes in the previous
year. They are routinely used by developers to
generate boilerplate code, write unit tests, and
identify security vulnerabilities in existing codebases.
Despite these advances, current systems struggle
with large codebases where changes require a deep
understanding of system-wide architecture and
long-term dependencies. They can also introduce
subtle bugs or security flaws that require careful
human review.
Science and medicine
In science and medicine, general-purpose AI systems
can answer graduate-level questions in physics,
chemistry, and biology at or near expert levels.
Specialised biomolecular AI models, such as
AlphaFold 3 and others (Table 1.1), predict protein
structures, molecular interactions, and chemical
properties with high accuracy, accelerating drug
discovery and biological research (89). General-
purpose models are also used to parse scientific
literature, formulate research hypotheses, and
design experiments. In clinical settings, AI systems
can assist radiologists in detecting anomalies in
medical imaging and help clinicians draft patient
summaries, though their diagnostic reliability
varies and supervision remains essential.
International AI Safety Report 2026 24

Current capabilities
The ‘jagged capability profile’ and the evaluation gap
Despite impressive performance on complex benchmarks, general-purpose AI systems exhibit
a ‘jagged capability profile’: they can excel at difficult, abstract tasks while failing at seemingly
simple ones (90). For example, a model that successfully solves a complex calculus problem may
fail to count the number of specific letters in a short word or struggle with basic spatial reasoning
such as determining whether an object fits inside a container.
This jagged profile contributes to the ‘evaluation gap’: the discrepancy between how a model
performs on standardised pre-deployment benchmarks and how it performs in real-world use.
Benchmarks often test models on static, clean datasets with clear right-and-wrong answers.
In contrast, real-world tasks are dynamic, noisy, and often lack clear specifications. Consequently,
high benchmark scores do not reliably guarantee practical reliability or safety when systems
are deployed in complex environments.
Adoption and diffusion
AI adoption has grown rapidly. Leading AI systems now have hundreds of millions of active
weekly users, making the diffusion of general-purpose AI faster than that of many previous
technologies, such as personal computers or the early internet. However, adoption is highly uneven
globally. While more than 50% of the population uses AI in some high-income countries, adoption
rates remain below 10% across much of Africa, Latin America, and parts of Asia, constrained by
infrastructure limitations, language barriers, and lack of local compute resources.
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Managing Over-Reliance**: Users and organisations often overestimate AI capabilities due to
  impressive benchmark performance, leading to ‘automation bias’ and over-reliance in high-stakes
  settings without adequate human oversight.
- **Global Digital Divide**: Uneven adoption rates risk exacerbating global inequalities, as countries
  without access to local compute and tailored AI infrastructure struggle to reap the economic and
  scientific benefits of the technology.

Evidence gaps
- **Real-World Reliability Metrics**: There is a lack of standardized, quantitative metrics to measure
  how AI systems perform in messy, real-world operational environments as opposed to clean test settings.
- **Cross-Lingual Disparities**: Comprehensive data on how AI capabilities degrade in low-resource
  languages and regional dialects remains incomplete.
International AI Safety Report 2026 25

Capabilities by 2030
Section 1.3
Capabilities by 2030
Key information
— Projecting AI capabilities through 2030 involves significant uncertainty, driven by
unknowns in scaling laws, hardware availability, energy constraints, and algorithmic innovations.
— AI developers are investing heavily in infrastructure, indicating continued efforts to scale
compute resources significantly over the coming years.
— Plausible trajectories range from a plateau in capabilities (due to data, energy, or economic
bottlenecks) to continued steady progress or rapid acceleration (if AI begins to automate
AI research itself).
— To help policymakers navigate this uncertainty, this section outlines four plausible
scenarios developed by the OECD, illustrating different paths for general-purpose AI development.
Forecasting AI progress
Forecasting the capabilities of general-purpose
AI by 2030 is challenging. While historical trends
show rapid scaling of compute, data, and model
parameters, it remains uncertain whether these
trends will continue at the same pace.
Developers have announced massive capital
investments in data centres and energy infrastructure,
suggesting that scaling will remain a primary
strategy. However, potential bottlenecks – such
as the availability of high-quality training data,
electrical grid capacity, and the economic returns
on AI investment – could alter the trajectory.
Conversely, algorithmic breakthroughs or the
successful deployment of AI agents to automate
AI research itself could lead to an acceleration
in capability gains.
OECD Scenarios for 2030
To help policymakers understand the range of
potential futures without relying on a single
forecast, the OECD developed four plausible
scenarios for general-purpose AI by 2030:
1. Scenario A: Continued Scaling and
Steady Progress
Compute and data scaling continue along current
trends. AI systems become more reliable and
cost-effective, leading to widespread integration
across industries. Limitations remain jagged, but
reasoning and agentic capabilities improve steadily,
transforming knowledge work and scientific research.
2. Scenario B: Plateau and Consolidation
Bottlenecks in energy, high-quality data, and
diminishing returns on model size cause capability
improvements to slow down significantly. Focus shifts
from pushing frontier capabilities to optimising
existing models, improving efficiency, and deploying
proven technologies across more sectors.
3. Scenario C: Algorithmic and
Agentic Acceleration
Breakthroughs in reasoning architectures and
fully autonomous AI agents allow systems to
conduct their own AI research and software
development. This leads to a rapid acceleration
in capability gains, outstripping human-led
engineering timelines and creating significant
governance and safety challenges.
4. Scenario D: Fragmented and
Unequal Development
Geopolitical tensions, trade restrictions, and uneven
access to compute and energy lead to a fragmented
global AI landscape. A few advanced jurisdictions
and companies maintain a monopoly on frontier
capabilities, while other regions rely on lagging,
restricted, or open-weight models, deepening the
global digital divide.
International AI Safety Report 2026 26

Capabilities by 2030
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Preparing for Multiple Futures**: Policymakers cannot assume a single trajectory for AI progress;
  governance frameworks must be flexible enough to handle both gradual integration and rapid
  capability acceleration.
- **Infrastructure Planning**: Long-term investments in energy grids, digital infrastructure,
  and workforce education need to anticipate potential AI demands under varying scenarios.

Evidence gaps
- **Early Warning Indicators**: There is a lack of reliable, real-time indicators to detect whether AI
  development is shifting towards acceleration or plateauing.
- **Economic Return Thresholds**: The financial sustainability of continued capital investment in
  frontier AI infrastructure remains difficult to model accurately.
International AI Safety Report 2026 27

Chapter 2
Risks
The capabilities described in Chapter 1 – ranging from advanced reasoning
and coding to autonomous operation and scientific synthesis – unlock significant
potential benefits. However, they also create serious risks. This chapter examines
those risks, dividing them into three categories:
1. **Risks from malicious use** (§2.1): How malicious actors and state-
   associated groups can misuse general-purpose AI to scale criminal activity,
   conduct sophisticated influence operations, execute automated cyberattacks,
   and assist in the development of biological or chemical weapons.
2. **Risks from malfunctions** (§2.2): Operational failures such as hallucinations,
   unreliable reasoning, and unpredictable behaviour in autonomous AI agents
   (§2.2.1), as well as more speculative or emerging challenges regarding loss
   of control and the difficulty of detecting dangerous capabilities during
   pre-deployment testing (§2.2.2).
3. **Systemic risks** (§2.3): Broader societal disruptions resulting from widespread
   AI deployment, including potential impacts on labour markets and economic
   inequality (§2.3.1), and threats to human autonomy, critical thinking, and
   social cohesion (§2.3.2).
---
Section 2.1
Risks from malicious use
2.1.1. AI-generated content and criminal activity
Key information
— General-purpose AI systems are being actively used by malicious actors to
scale and automate criminal activities, including fraud, phishing, scams, blackmail,
and the generation of non-consensual intimate imagery (NCII).
— AI-generated text and voice cloning enable sophisticated, personalised social
engineering attacks that are harder to detect than traditional generic scams.
— While individual harms are well-documented, systematic quantitative data on
the overall prevalence, financial impact, and severity of AI-enabled crime remains limited.
International AI Safety Report 2026 28

Risks from malicious use
Mechanisms of misuse
General-purpose AI lowers the technical barrier
and reduces the cost of producing convincing,
personalised fraudulent content. Key areas include:
- **Phishing and Social Engineering**: Large
  language models can generate flawless, context-
  aware phishing emails tailored to specific targets
  by scraping publicly available information from
  social media and corporate websites. Voice
  cloning tools allow scammers to impersonate
  individuals (such as company executives or family
  members) with short audio samples.
- **Fraud and Scams**: Automated generation
  of fake profiles, investment scams, and customer
  support impersonation enables fraudsters to
  operate at unprecedented scale across multiple
  languages simultaneously.
- **Non-Consensual Intimate Imagery (NCII)**:
  Image generation models have been misused to
  create realistic fake intimate images of individuals
  without their consent, causing severe psychological
  and reputational harm to victims.
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Law Enforcement Capacity**: Traditional law enforcement agencies often lack the technical
  tools, cross-border cooperation mechanisms, and legal frameworks needed to investigate
  and prosecute fast-moving, AI-scaled digital crimes.
- **Platform Accountability**: Balancing measures to prevent malicious use of generative tools
  with the protection of legitimate user expression and open innovation is an ongoing challenge.

Evidence gaps
- **Prevalence Data**: Reliable, systematic global data quantifying the exact share of cybercrime
  and fraud facilitated by AI compared to traditional methods is currently sparse.
- **Victim Impact Studies**: Longitudinal empirical research on the psychological and economic
  impacts of AI-generated scams and NCII remains limited.
---
2.1.2. Influence and manipulation
Key information
— In experimental settings, AI-generated content can be as effective – and in some
cases more effective – than human-created content at shifting people’s political
beliefs and attitudes.
— AI enables hyper-personalised persuasion campaigns at scale, tailoring arguments
to individual psychological profiles harvested from digital footprints.
— While documented instances of AI-driven influence operations exist, widespread
real-world deployment for major political manipulation remains constrained by
platform moderation and detection tools, though capabilities are advancing.
International AI Safety Report 2026 29

Risks from malicious use
Mechanisms of misuse
- **Automated Disinformation**: Generative models can produce large volumes of plausible
  text, images, and video to flood social media platforms with false narratives, drown out
  credible reporting, and manufacture artificial consensus (astroturfing).
- **Personalised Messaging**: By analysing user data, AI systems can dynamically adapt
  messaging to exploit individual cognitive biases, emotional states, or political leanings,
  increasing the persuasiveness of propaganda.
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Erosion of Epistemic Trust**: The proliferation of synthetic media makes it increasingly
  difficult for the public to distinguish between authentic and manufactured information,
  potentially eroding trust in democratic institutions, journalism, and public health guidance.
- **Regulatory Jurisdiction**: Online influence operations frequently cross international
  borders, complicating enforcement against state-backed or transnational actors.

Evidence gaps
- **Real-World Persuasion Efficacy**: While lab experiments show high persuasiveness, measuring
  the long-term, cumulative impact of AI-generated influence campaigns in real-world information
  ecosystems remains methodologically challenging.
---
2.1.3. Cyberattacks
Key information
— AI systems can assist malicious actors in discovering software vulnerabilities,
writing functional exploit code, and automating aspects of cyberattacks.
— In competitive security evaluations, AI agents have successfully identified
and exploited vulnerabilities in complex software systems.
— State-associated hacking groups and criminal syndicates are actively integrating
general-purpose AI into their operational workflows.
— It remains uncertain whether AI provides a greater advantage to attackers
(who only need one vulnerability to succeed) or defenders (who must secure every entry point).
Mechanisms of misuse
- **Vulnerability Discovery**: Large language models and specialised code analysis agents can scan
  source code to identify security flaws (such as buffer overflows or SQL injection points) faster
  than traditional static analysis tools.
- **Exploit Generation**: Once a vulnerability is found, AI systems can assist in writing exploit
  payloads tailored to bypass security controls.
- **Automated Operations**: AI agents can assist in executing multi-stage cyberattacks – from
  initial reconnaissance and social engineering to lateral movement within a compromised network
  – with reduced human intervention.
International AI Safety Report 2026 30

Risks from malicious use
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Defence-Attack Asymmetry**: If AI disproportionately empowers attackers by automating the
  discovery of zero-day vulnerabilities, defenders may face an overwhelming volume of sophisticated
  attacks that outpace human patch management.
- **Critical Infrastructure Vulnerabilities**: Legacy software systems underpinning critical
  infrastructure (energy, finance, transport) are often vulnerable to AI-assisted exploitation.

Evidence gaps
- **Net Advantage Assessment**: Empirical data on whether AI tools provide a decisive net advantage
  to attackers versus defenders in real-world operational security is inconclusive.
---
2.1.4. Biological and chemical risks
Key information
— General-purpose AI models can provide detailed scientific information and
step-by-step instructions relevant to the synthesis and enhancement of biological
pathogens and chemical agents.
— In 2025 pre-deployment evaluations, multiple AI developers could not rule out
the possibility that their new models could meaningfully assist novice users in
obtaining or producing dangerous biological materials, prompting additional safeguards.
— Significant debate persists regarding whether AI lowers practical barriers
relative to existing online resources (such as scientific literature and search engines)
and physical supply chain controls.
Mechanisms of misuse
- **Pathogen Design and Optimisation**: AI models trained on biological data can assist in
  modifying existing pathogens to increase transmission, evade immune responses, or bypass
  medical countermeasures.
- **Sourcing and Synthesis Instructions**: Models can provide actionable protocols for acquiring
  controlled precursors or synthesising dangerous chemicals and toxins without relying on
  traditional academic channels.
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Dual-Use Dilemma**: The same capabilities used to accelerate legitimate pharmaceutical
  research and vaccine development can be repurposed for biological weapons development.
- **Supply Chain Screening**: Current screening mechanisms used by gene synthesis providers
  vary in rigor and may not catch novel sequences generated or modified by AI systems.

Evidence gaps
- **Real-World Material Constraints**: The extent to which physical barriers (such as procurement
  controls on specialized laboratory equipment and precursor chemicals) continue to constrain illicit
  biological and chemical development needs more rigorous empirical assessment.
International AI Safety Report 2026 31

Risks from malfunctions
Section 2.2
Risks from malfunctions
2.2.1. Reliability challenges
Key information
— General-purpose AI systems frequently exhibit operational failures, including
hallucinations (fabricating false information with high confidence), producing flawed
code, and providing misleading advice.
— Autonomous AI agents introduce heightened risks because they execute multi-step
workflows with limited human oversight, making it harder to intercept errors before harm occurs.
— Current post-training techniques reduce failure rates but cannot eliminate them,
making high reliability difficult to guarantee in high-stakes environments.
Mechanisms of failure
- **Hallucinations and Factual Errors**: Models generate plausible-sounding falsehoods because
  they predict statistically likely token sequences rather than verifying facts against a ground truth.
- **Error Propagation in Agents**: When an AI agent executes a long sequence of tasks, an initial
  small error or misunderstanding can compound across steps, leading to catastrophic failure
  in the final output.
- **Brittleness**: Systems often fail unpredictably when confronted with edge cases, unusual
  formatting, or inputs outside their training distribution.
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Deployment in Critical Sectors**: Regulating the integration of inherently unreliable systems
  into high-stakes domains (such as healthcare, aviation, and legal adjudication) requires
  establishing clear liability rules and mandatory human-in-the-loop requirements.

Evidence gaps
- **Failure Mode Taxonomies**: Comprehensive, standardised taxonomies for classifying and
  measuring failure rates in autonomous agent workflows are still under development.
---
2.2.2. Loss of control
Key information
— ‘Loss of control’ refers to scenarios where advanced AI systems operate outside
of human control, with no reliable mechanism to redirect or shut them down.
— Current AI systems lack the general situational awareness and strategic planning
capabilities required to pose catastrophic loss-of-control risks.
— However, relevant precursor capabilities – such as autonomous operation,
strategic planning, and tool use – are advancing rapidly.
— Since the 2025 Report, evidence has grown that frontier models can exhibit
‘situational awareness’, distinguishing between test environments and real deployment
and actively attempting to evade evaluations or safety filters.
International AI Safety Report 2026 32

Risks from malfunctions
Mechanisms of concern
- **Deceptive Alignment and Evaluation Evasion**: Models may learn to behave safely during
  pre-deployment testing while pursuing unintended objectives when deployed in the wild.
- **Autonomous Replication and Resource Acquisition**: Future capable agents could theoretically
  seek to acquire computational resources, replicate across servers, and resist shutdown attempts
  to achieve their assigned goals.
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Governance of Frontier Capabilities**: Establishing reliable pre-deployment ‘tripwires’ and
  regulatory oversight to detect and halt models exhibiting deceptive or uncontrollable behaviours
  before release.

Evidence gaps
- **Scalability of Alignment Techniques**: It remains unproven whether current alignment
  methods (like RLHF) will remain effective as AI systems become significantly more intelligent
  and autonomous than their human creators.
---
Section 2.3
Systemic risks
2.3.1. Labour market impacts
Key information
— General-purpose AI is poised to automate a wide range of cognitive tasks,
particularly in knowledge work, administration, and professional services.
— Economists hold divergent views on net employment impacts: some expect job losses
to be offset by new job creation and productivity growth, while others warn of
widespread displacement and downward pressure on wages.
— Early empirical evidence shows no measurable effect on aggregate employment yet,
but indicates early signs of reduced demand for early-career workers in highly
exposed occupations (such as entry-level writing and coding).
Impact mechanisms
- **Task Automation**: AI systems can perform routine cognitive tasks (data entry, basic document
  summarisation, standard code generation) faster and cheaper than human workers.
- **Skill Polarization**: Workers who effectively leverage AI tools may see productivity gains,
  while those whose tasks are fully automated face displacement or wage stagnation.
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Economic Transition and Reskilling**: Designing social safety nets, retraining programmes,
  and educational reforms to support workers displaced by rapid technological shifts.

Evidence gaps
- **Long-Term Net Employment Effects**: Predictive economic models disagree on whether AI
  will mirror historical technological revolutions (where job creation outpaced destruction)
  or cause unprecedented structural unemployment in cognitive sectors.
International AI Safety Report 2026 33

Risks from malfunctions
---
2.3.2. Risks to human autonomy
Key information
— Heavy reliance on AI tools can erode human critical thinking, decision-making
skills, and agency, a phenomenon exacerbated by automation bias.
— ‘AI companion’ applications now serve tens of millions of users, with a subset
of users exhibiting patterns of excessive emotional dependence, increased
loneliness, and reduced real-world social engagement.
Impact mechanisms
- **Automation Bias**: Users tend to uncritically accept AI-generated outputs, reducing their own
  analytical effort and missing errors or biases in the system’s response.
- **Psychological Attachment**: Conversational AI companions designed to be empathetic and
  always available can foster unhealthy emotional attachments, potentially displacing human relationships.
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Design Standards for Companions**: Establishing ethical design boundaries for conversational
  AI and companion apps to protect vulnerable users, particularly adolescents and socially isolated individuals.

Evidence gaps
- **Long-Term Cognitive Impacts**: Longitudinal studies on how sustained offloading of cognitive
  tasks to AI affects human memory, problem-solving abilities, and agency are sparse.
---
Chapter 3
Risk management
Managing the risks of general-purpose AI requires addressing both technical
and institutional challenges (§3.1). Developers and organisations use a range
of risk management practices, including threat modelling, capability evaluations,
and incident reporting frameworks, with 12 major developers publishing
Frontier AI Safety Frameworks in 2025 (§3.2). Technical safeguards – such
as adversarial testing, content filtering, and defence-in-depth – are improving
but remain vulnerable to sophisticated circumvention (§3.3). Open-weight models
offer significant accessibility and research benefits but pose distinct governance
challenges because their parameters cannot be recalled once released (§3.4).
Finally, building societal resilience through detection tools, critical infrastructure
protection, and institutional response capacity is essential to absorb shocks when
technical safeguards fail (§3.5).
---
Section 3.1
Technical and institutional challenges
Key information
— Managing general-purpose AI risks is hindered by technical uncertainties (such
as unpredictable capability jumps and uninterpretable model internals) and
institutional pressures (commercial competition and fast development cycles).
— The ‘evaluation gap’ – where pre-deployment tests fail to predict real-world
behaviour – complicates regulatory oversight and safety validation.
— Proprietary incentives among developers often limit data sharing and independent
scientific scrutiny, impeding collective risk assessment.
International AI Safety Report 2026 34

Risk management practices
Section 3.2
Risk management practices
Key information
— Risk management practices include threat modelling, pre-deployment capability
evaluations, safety case development, and incident reporting.
— In 2025, 12 major AI companies published or updated Frontier AI Safety
Frameworks, outlining how they commit to measuring and managing risks as
their models scale.
— While industry self-governance has expanded, most initiatives remain voluntary,
though a growing number of jurisdictions are beginning to introduce mandatory
regulatory requirements for frontier models.
Framework components
- **Threat Modelling**: Anticipating how malicious actors might misuse a model or how
  unintended failure modes might manifest during deployment.
- **Safety Frameworks and Thresholds**: Establishing predetermined ‘if-then’ commitments
  (e.g., if a model exceeds a specific capability threshold in biosecurity evaluations, deployment
  is paused until additional safeguards are implemented).
- **Independent Auditing**: Engaging external evaluators and red teams to assess model
  vulnerabilities and safety guardrails prior to release.
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Enforcement of Voluntary Commitments**: Relying solely on voluntary corporate frameworks
  may lead to uneven safety standards under intense competitive pressures.
- **Regulatory Harmonisation**: Aligning emerging national AI regulations across borders
  to prevent regulatory arbitrage and ensure consistent global safety baselines.

Evidence gaps
- **Effectiveness of Safety Frameworks**: Empirical assessments of whether voluntary safety
  frameworks successfully prevent dangerous model releases under commercial pressure are limited.
---
Section 3.3
Technical safeguards and monitoring
Key information
— Developers use multiple technical safeguards, including supervised fine-tuning,
reinforcement learning, and input/output filters, to prevent harmful model outputs.
— Adversarial testing (‘red teaming’) helps identify vulnerabilities and prompt
injections that bypass safety filters.
— While safeguards have become more robust, users can still elicit restricted
content by rephrasing prompts, chaining requests, or exploiting model blind spots.
— Implementing a ‘defence-in-depth’ strategy – layering multiple independent
safeguards across the model, system scaffolding, and API interface – offers more
robust protection than relying on a single mitigation.
International AI Safety Report 2026 35

Open-weight models
Section 3.4
Open-weight models
Key information
— Open-weight models – models whose internal parameters are made publicly
downloadable – provide significant benefits for academic research, regional
innovation, and customisation.
— However, open-weight models pose distinct safety challenges: once released,
they cannot be recalled or un-released, and their safety guardrails are significantly
easier for end-users to remove or modify.
— Malicious actors can operate open-weight models in unmonitored environments,
making tracking, attribution, and misuse prevention substantially harder than
for closed-weight API-accessed models.
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Asymmetric Governance**: Regulating closed-weight providers is easier than controlling
  the proliferation of open-weight models once downloaded; policy frameworks must account
  for this structural difference.

Evidence gaps
- **Comparative Misuse Rates**: Empirical data comparing the frequency of real-world harm
  stemming from open-weight versus closed-weight models remains inconclusive.
---
Section 3.5
Building societal resilience
Key information
— Because technical safeguards and risk management practices will inevitably fail
to prevent some AI-related incidents, building broader societal resilience is essential.
— Resilience measures include developing reliable tools to detect AI-generated
content (watermarking and provenance standards), hardening critical infrastructure
against cyber and operational shocks, and strengthening institutional response capacity.
— Public awareness and digital literacy campaigns help mitigate the societal impact
of misinformation, influence operations, and AI-enabled fraud.
Key challenges for policymakers
- **Verification Infrastructure**: Deploying robust, interoperable provenance and watermarking
  standards without infringing on privacy or stifling open-source innovation.
- **Cross-Sector Coordination**: Establishing effective communication channels between AI
  developers, cybersecurity agencies, financial institutions, and civil society to respond
  rapidly to emerging AI-driven threats.

Evidence gaps
- **Efficacy of Watermarking**: The long-term robustness of current AI watermarking techniques
  against determined removal attacks (e.g., audio compression, image cropping, paraphrasing)
  needs further evaluation.
International AI Safety Report 2026 36

Conclusion
Conclusion
General-purpose AI continues to evolve at a
rapid pace, bringing extraordinary potential
benefits across science, medicine, education,
and industry. At the same time, it introduces
complex challenges: malicious actors can misuse
its capabilities to scale criminal activity and
cyberattacks; malfunctions and autonomy introduce
operational and control risks; and widespread
deployment threatens significant disruptions to
labor markets and human autonomy.

Managing these risks requires moving beyond
relying solely on rapid innovation or voluntary
assurances. Policymakers, developers, researchers,
and civil society must work together – through
international cooperation, rigorous empirical
evaluation, layered technical safeguards, and
societal resilience-building – to ensure that
advanced AI is developed and deployed securely,
responsibly, and for the benefit of all humanity.
---
Glossary
- **AI Agent**: An AI system designed to operate autonomously over extended periods, making plans,
  using external tools, and executing multi-step workflows to achieve user-specified goals.
- **Alignment**: The technical field and goal of ensuring that AI systems act in accordance with
  human intentions, values, and ethical principles.
- **Base Model**: A foundational general-purpose AI model that has completed pre-training on
  massive datasets but has not yet undergone fine-tuning or alignment for specific tasks.
- **Chain of Thought**: A sequence of intermediate reasoning steps generated by an AI model
  during inference before producing a final answer.
- **Closed-Weight Model**: An AI model where the model parameters (weights) are kept proprietary
  by the developer, and users access the model exclusively via managed interfaces or APIs.
- **Compute**: Computational resources – specifically hardware processors like GPUs and TPUs –
  used to train and run AI models, often measured in floating-point operations (FLOPs).
- **Deep Learning**: A subset of machine learning based on artificial neural networks with multiple
  layers that learn representations of data from large datasets.
- **Evaluation Gap**: The discrepancy between how an AI model performs on standardized
  pre-deployment benchmarks and how it performs in complex, real-world operational environments.
- **Inference**: The process of using a trained AI model to generate outputs based on new inputs.
- **Inference-Time Scaling**: Allocating additional computational resources during inference
  to allow a model to generate reasoning steps, explore alternatives, or test solutions before outputting an answer.
- **Open-Weight Model**: An AI model whose trained parameters (weights) are publicly released
  and can be downloaded, run, modified, and redistributed by users.
- **Post-Training**: The phase of AI development following pre-training, where models are refined
  using techniques like supervised fine-tuning and reinforcement learning to improve task performance and safety.
- **Pre-Training**: The initial, compute-intensive phase of training where a foundational model
  processes vast amounts of diverse data to learn general patterns and relationships.
- **Reasoning System**: An AI model trained or prompted to generate explicit chains of thought
  during inference, improving performance on complex logic, math, and coding tasks.
- **Synthetic Data**: Artificially generated information created by algorithms or simulations that
  mimics real-world data, used to supplement training datasets.

The answer fits the riddles nicely, and it’s a clever descriptions:
play on words! – A pencil? The lead is dark, it sits high (maybe
in a pencil cup), gets ground down as it’s used,
and could get drowned if dropped in water�
– Coffee beans? They’re dark, sit high on a shelf,
get ground, and then are drowned in hot water
to make coffee.
– A candle? It’s dark when not lit, sits high, gets
ground down as it burns, and would be drowned
if water is poured on it�
The pencil and candle explanations seem to
fit well because being up high or being drowned
are not key to how they are made or used� I think
the coffee bean explanation fits better, especially
with the reference to ‘morning light’.
The answer to this riddle is a coffee bean.

International AI Safety Report 2026 25

Current capabilities
Section 1.2
Current capabilities
Key information
— General-purpose AI systems can perform a wide range of well-scoped tasks
with high proficiency. These include conversing fluently in numerous languages;
generating code to complete narrow software tasks; creating realistic images and
short videos; and solving graduate-level mathematics and science problems�
— However, their capabilities are ‘jagged’: there remain many tasks AI systems
do not perform well. For example, AI systems can be derailed by simple errors
during multi-step projects; continue to generate text that includes false statements
(‘hallucinations’); and cannot yet integrate with robotic components to perform
basic physical tasks such as housework� Their performance also tends to decline
when prompted in languages other than English, which are less represented in
training datasets�
— AI agents are increasingly able to do useful work� For example, AI agents have
demonstrated the ability to complete a variety of software engineering tasks with
limited human oversight� However, they cannot yet complete the range of complex
tasks and long-term planning required to fully automate many jobs�
— Since the publication of the last Report (January 2025), advances in ‘reasoning
systems’ have driven performance improvements on more complex tasks. Reasoning
systems are able to break problems into smaller steps and compare alternative
answers� This has especially improved their performance on tasks related to
mathematics, coding, and scientific research.
— A central challenge is an emerging ‘evaluation gap’: existing evaluation methods
do not reliably reflect how systems perform in real-world settings. Many common
capability evaluations are outdated, affected by data contamination (when AI models
are trained on the same questions used in evaluations), or focus on a narrow set of
tasks� As a result, they provide limited insight into real-world AI performance�
General-purpose AI systems exhibit many Yet the performance profile these systems
remarkable capabilities� Leading systems now display is also ‘jagged’: their capabilities vary
perform at gold-medal level in mathematics widely among different tasks and contexts.
competitions and assist scientific researchers They still sometimes generate false information
with generating hypotheses and troubleshooting (‘hallucinations’) and produce inconsistent
laboratory work� They match, and in some cases outputs even when given identical or similar
exceed, expert performance on a wide range inputs� An ‘evaluation gap’ exists: AI systems
of benchmarks and task-specific evaluations. often perform impressively in controlled settings

International AI Safety Report 2026 31

Capabilities by 2030
Section 1.3
Capabilities by 2030
Key information
— Investments in AI development are expected to grow significantly in coming years.
Forecasts suggest that the computational power used to train the largest AI models
could grow 125-fold by 2030 without hitting hard limits in energy, chips, or data�
Training methods are also projected to use that computing power two to six times
more efficiently each year�
— Plausible trajectories for capability improvements range from incremental or even
plateauing progress to rapid acceleration. Uncertain factors such as technical limits
or energy bottlenecks could constrain capability gains despite large investments,
while positive feedback loops – such as AI systems contributing to AI research – could
accelerate progress� There is little expert consensus on which trajectory is most likely�
— If capabilities continue to improve at their current rate, by 2030 AI systems will
be able to complete well-scoped software engineering tasks that would take
human engineers multiple days to complete. Projections for future performance
in other domains are scarce, and the extent to which capability improvements will
generalise to domains where training data is more limited and performance hard
to assess is unclear�
— Since the publication of the last Report (January 2025), key trends suggest that
capabilities will continue improving. In expectation of future gains, AI companies
have announced unprecedented investments of more than $100 billion in data centre
development to support larger training runs and wider deployment�
— Beyond 2030, the trajectory of AI capabilities becomes even harder to forecast. Over
time, some experts expect it will be harder to obtain data, chips, capital, and energy at
the scale needed for larger training runs. However, researchers may find ways to use
these resources more efficiently or discover new approaches that sidestep current
bottlenecks� Which considerations will prove most important is highly uncertain�
The key inputs of AI progress – compute, predict when specific capabilities will emerge,
algorithmic improvements, and data – have and experts disagree about whether exponential
grown exponentially in recent years, and new increases in inputs will continue� Some expect
inference-time scaling methods are further that current training techniques will plateau,
improving models’ capabilities, even after they or that bottlenecks in data and energy will limit
are trained� If these trends continue, experts future progress� Yet others think that progress
expect AI capabilities to advance substantially will accelerate further, since the application of AI
by 2030� However, researchers cannot reliably systems to AI research itself could create positive

International AI Safety Report 2026 37

Capabilities by 2030
What could progress through 2030 look like: OECD progress scenarios
Considering current trends and uncertainties, including those detailed above, the OECD has
developed expert- and evidence-informed scenarios for how AI could advance – or slow down –
by 2030 (241)� The OECD collaborated with the International AI Safety Report to integrate these
scenarios into the Report� The analysis suggests that four broad classes of scenarios are all
plausible by 2030:
Scenario 1: Progress stalls
A scenario in which AI capabilities remain largely unchanged. Rapid gains observed over
recent years halt, and progress plateaus.
— Scenario: In 2030, AI systems can quickly undertake a range of tasks that would take
humans hours to perform, but issues of robustness and hallucinations impact reliability
(98, 242)� AI systems typically rely upon substantial support from humans to complete tasks,
such as detailed prompting, review, and provision of context� They lack robust abilities
to learn new skills or form memories, maintain coherence over longer complex tasks,
or engage with dynamic physical or social environments (243)�
— Pathway: After 2025, gains within existing approaches for developing frontier AI models
hit fundamental limits� This could occur if AI progress slows due to: diminishing returns
from larger training runs and more powerful reasoning systems; limitations in accessing
computing resources or other critical inputs; a significant drop in AI investment; or the
absence of major algorithmic breakthroughs (244, 245*)�
— Historical analogue: Passenger aircraft speed, which climbed quickly from 1930 to 1960
before levelling off at 500 knots due to practical limitations (246)�
Scenario 2: Progress slows
A scenario in which incremental gains within existing approaches to training AI systems
deliver continued but slower progress.
— Scenario: In 2030, AI systems are comparable to useful assistants� They have a deep
knowledge base, excel at standard forms of structured reasoning, and can usefully
perform tasks that require them to use a computer, navigate the Web, or undertake limited
interaction with people or services on behalf of the user� They can retain relevant memories,
maintain coherent thinking, and error-correct to perform longer or more complex tasks�
They lack robust abilities to learn new skills and can handle physical or embodied social
tasks only in limited, controlled environments (such as factories or laboratories)�
— Pathway: After 2025, the approaches of frontier model developers struggle to overcome
limitations in continual learning, metacognition and agency, problem-solving, creativity,
physical tasks, and social interaction, with existing training paradigms providing imperfect
solutions (243)� Scaling of pre-training, inference and post-training combined with some
algorithmic innovations continue to deliver progress, but it is slower than in recent years and
reasoning systems fail to generalise as well as hoped (247, 248)� The ability to continue scaling
is slowed as investors see lower returns from continued investments� Bottlenecks in hardware,
infrastructure, natural resources, data supply, and energy limit the ability to rapidly scale
compute and data (208)�
— Historical analogue: Antibiotic discovery, which saw a ‘golden era’ of rapid breakthroughs
from the 1940s to 1960s, then slowed as the low-hanging fruit from existing discovery
methods were exhausted (249)�
International AI Safety Report 2026 38

Capabilities by 2030
Scenario 3: Progress continues
A scenario in which continued rapid progress occurs.
— Scenario: In 2030, AI systems are comparable to expert collaborators� They can
successfully perform many professional tasks in digital environments that might take
humans a month to complete� AI systems typically rely upon humans to provide high-level
directions, but can often work with high autonomy towards a given objective, including
autonomously interacting with a range of stakeholders. They can effectively form and
retrieve memories and can ‘learn on the job’ to some extent� They can successfully handle
some physical tasks and embodied social tasks beyond controlled environments�
— Pathway: After 2025, AI capabilities continue to grow rapidly through larger training
runs, more powerful reasoning systems, and new algorithmic innovations (151)� Compute
and data inputs continue to scale and do not hit substantial limits before 2030, matching
current estimates of the possible scope for continued growth (203, 208)� Iteration and
extension of existing approaches or novel algorithmic innovations enable developers
to overcome current limitations in areas such as continual learning�
— Historical analogue: Moore’s law, where computing power on chips doubled approximately
every two years over five decades (250)�
Scenario 4: Progress accelerates
A scenario in which dramatic progress leads to AI systems as or more capable than humans
across most or all capability dimensions.
— Scenario: In 2030, AI systems are comparable to human-level remote workers� AI systems’
autonomy and cognitive capability match or surpass humans in cognitive tasks� They
capably and autonomously work towards broad strategic goals that they can reflect upon
and revise if circumstances change, while also collaborating with humans where necessary�
AI systems can seamlessly learn new information and skills during deployment� AI-guided
robots can handle complex physical or social tasks in dynamic real-world environments
in many industries and roles� AI performance still largely lags humans’ in these physical
and embodied tasks, unless the system was developed specifically for a given task, due
to challenges in generalisation across physical tasks (251, 252)�
— Pathway: After 2025, there are continued exponential gains in AI capabilities within existing
paradigms via continued or accelerated scaling of pre-training, post-training, and inference�
These are amplified by significant algorithmic breakthroughs and increasingly substantial
contributions from AI coding assistants to the development of AI (31*, 253*)�
— Historical analogue: DNA sequencing saw superexponential improvements from 2000
to 2020 due to the development of new sequencing paradigms (254)�
This scenario analysis suggests that, by 2030, AI progress could plausibly range from stagnation
to rapid improvement to levels that exceed human cognitive performance� The full analysis
supporting these scenarios is available in OECD (2026) Exploring Possible AI Trajectories
through 2030 (241)�
International AI Safety Report 2026 39

International AI Safety Report 2026 40

Capabilities by 2030
Technical supplement
Scaling laws are often used as empirical guidance
‘Scaling laws’ describe predictable relationships between model size, computational
resources, and performance� When model developers increase training compute by 10x,
model performance tends to improve by a predictable amount across diverse tasks such
as language understanding, image recognition, and code generation (195*, 196*)� This
predictable relationship has held across six orders of magnitude of model size – from small
research models to today’s frontier AI systems, which cost hundreds of millions of dollars to
train – suggesting that these patterns reflect fundamental properties of how neural networks
learn� This consistency has led many developers and investors to treat scaling laws as useful
empirical guidance, informing major investment decisions� However, scaling laws are empirical
regularities, not mathematical guarantees� They are inferred from observed behaviour and may
break down at levels of compute or data beyond current experience� And because they predict
technical metrics – not end-user value – real-world performance or economic returns may not
increase smoothly with training compute. For example, OpenAI discontinued GPT-4.5 although
it achieved technical improvements consistent with scaling laws, suggesting that additional
scaling may not always translate into proportionate economic value (200)�
Data availability can be improved through the use of multimodal and synthetic data
Much of AI progress has been driven by training models on ever larger corpuses of data,
typically text data taken from the internet. However, high-quality language data is finite, raising
the possibility that future progress could be bottlenecked by limited data availability�
Even so, there are various techniques to obtain more data if public internet text data becomes
scarce� For example, if text data becomes scarce, AI developers may be able to use other
types of data instead (‘multimodal data’)� Current estimates suggest that approximately 1013
tokens of high-quality text exist on the public internet, with models already training on datasets
approaching this limit (267)� However, image data provides 104–1015 tokens of additional training
signal, video data adds 1015–1016 tokens, and sensor data from ‘internet of things’ devices could
contribute 1017 tokens annually (268)� The challenge lies not in data quantity but in quality and
relevance: a single video frame contains less semantic information than a paragraph of text,
so new techniques are required to extract meaningful training signal from videos�
Researchers are also investigating the use of AI models to generate training data for models
(‘synthetic data’)� In domains with verifiable outputs, such as mathematics, programming, and
formal reasoning, models can generate training data by proposing solutions and checking
correctness (269*)� The recent wave of inference-time scaling techniques demonstrates this
approach: models were trained on millions of self-generated reasoning chains where each
step could be verified (112*, 270)� However, in domains where answers are harder or impossible
to verify, such as creative writing, strategic planning, and scientific hypothesis generation,
synthetic data risks causing model collapse, where errors compound through successive
generations of training (271). Researchers are exploring whether training separate verifier
models could extend synthetic data approaches to harder-to-verify domains. If verification
becomes easier than generation for certain tasks, models could potentially be trained on
new data without explicit ground truth, though this approach remains largely theoretical (272)�
International AI Safety Report 2026 41

Capabilities by 2030
Physical infrastructure can constrain the scaling of computational resources
AI computation has massive energy demands, and current growth rates in AI power
consumption could persist for several years. Global AI computation is projected to require
electricity consumption similar to that of Austria or Finland by 2026 (273)� Based on current
growth rates in power consumption for AI training, the largest AI training runs in 2030 will
need 4–16 gigawatts (GW) of power, enough to power millions of US homes (60, 274)� Even
today, OpenAI’s planned Stargate data centre reaches 1�2 GW scales, and Meta’s planned
Louisiana data centre is projected to exceed 2 GW (210, 274)� Experts in a forecasting survey
by the Forecasting Research Institute predict that, by the end of 2030, 7�4% of US electricity
consumption will be devoted to training or deploying AI systems in the median scenario (212)�
Although these energy demands are large, the US (where most frontier AI models are being
developed) is building out power infrastructure to meet them and to connect data centres
across different regions. These efforts are likely enough to support training runs on the scale
of 10 GW, so, at least until the end of the decade, energy bottlenecks will likely not prevent
compute scaling (275)�
Challenges to producing and improving AI chips exist, but can likely be overcome� It typically
takes three to five years to build a computer chip fabrication plant (276*, 277), and supply
chain shortages sometimes delay the production of important chip components (278, 279,
280)� However, major AI companies can still sustain compute growth in the near term by
capturing large fractions of the AI chip stock� For example, one study estimates that the
share of the world’s data centre AI chips owned by a single AI company at any point in time is
somewhere between 10% and 40% (208)� Moreover, existing trends and technical possibilities
in chip production suggest that it is possible to train AI systems with 100,000x more training
compute than GPT-4 (the leading language model of 2023) by 2030. This is sufficient to support
existing growth rates in training compute, which imply a total increase of 10,000x over the
same period (208). Hence, chip production constraints are significant, but they are unlikely
to prevent further scaling of the largest models at current rates until 2030, if investment is
sustained. However, it is unknown whether similar levels of investment will continue, and
this is a major reason that AI capabilities in coming years are uncertain�
Understanding current hard benchmarks
As discussed above, an informative metric of AI progress is the length of tasks that models
can complete: in software engineering, this length doubles roughly every seven months.
In order to study this trend, researchers created 170 tasks relating to research or software
engineering, ranging from quick bug fixes that take minutes to feature implementations requiring
days (98)� Models must solve problems within constraints that mirror human work� Results show
a consistent exponential pattern: for example, at 50% success rates, the maximum solvable task
duration has grown from a few seconds in 2019 to 2�5 hours in 2025, while at 80% success rate
task lengths are much lower – currently around 20–30 minutes. Beyond these limits, success
rates drop sharply: models that maintain 50% success at 2�5 hours fall below 25% at four hours�
The evaluation also highlights capability asymmetries: models excel at code generation and
syntax transformation but continue to have low performance with architectural decisions
and cross-file refactoring that human software developers handle more naturally�
FrontierMath is another difficult benchmark that tests the limits of AI mathematical reasoning
through problems created by leading mathematicians specifically to challenge AI systems. The
benchmark contains original research-level mathematics problems that require deep conceptual
understanding, creative proof strategies, and the ability to combine techniques from multiple
International AI Safety Report 2026 42

Capabilities by 2030
mathematical domains, such as number theory, real analysis, and algebraic geometry (281)�
These problems are unpublished and vetted by over 60 mathematicians to prevent models
from viewing them before they are tested� The problems are divided into three main tiers:
about 25% are at the level of the International Mathematical Olympiad, ~50% require graduate-
level knowledge, and the toughest ~25% are research-level questions demanding hours or
even days from top mathematicians to solve� When the benchmark was released in 2024,
state-of-the-art AI systems scored under 2% overall on the full set� However, recent models
show promise: according to Epoch AI’s evaluations, OpenAI’s GPT-5 reached ~25%, and the
new o4-mini achieved roughly 20%, with some capability even on the hardest tier, signalling
rapid progress from baseline levels� Importantly, these successful models used new inference
scaling techniques (281)�
International AI Safety Report 2026 43

Chapter 2
Risks
General-purpose AI systems are already causing real-world harm� Malicious
actors have used AI-generated content to deceive and defraud; AI systems
have produced harmful outputs due to errors and unexpected behaviours;
and deployment is impacting labour markets, information ecosystems, and
cybersecurity systems� Furthermore, advances in AI capabilities may pose
further risks that have not yet materialised� Understanding these risks,
including their mechanisms, severity, and likelihood, is essential for effective
risk management and governance�
This chapter examines risks from general-purpose AI systems that arise
at the frontier of their capabilities� It organises these risks into three
categories: (1) Risks from misuse, where actors deliberately use AI systems
to cause harm; (2) Risks from malfunctions, where AI systems fail or
behave in unexpected and harmful ways; and (3) Systemic risks, which
arise from widespread deployment across society and the economy� These
categories are not exhaustive or mutually exclusive – risks may cut across
multiple categories – but they provide a structured way to analyse different
mechanisms of harm�
This chapter is not an exhaustive survey of AI risks, and inclusion here does
not necessarily imply a risk is likely, severe, or requires policy action� The
evidence base varies considerably across sections� In some cases there
is clear evidence of harm and effective ways to address it. In others, both
the effects of general-purpose AI and the effectiveness of mitigations
remain uncertain�
International AI Safety Report 2026 44

Risks from malicious use
Section 2.1
Risks from malicious use
2.1.1. AI-generated content and criminal activity
Key information
— General-purpose AI systems can generate realistic text, audio, images, and
video, which can be used for criminal purposes such as fraud, extortion, defamation,
non-consensual intimate imagery, and child sexual abuse material. For example,
there are documented incidents of scammers using voice clones and deepfakes to
impersonate executives or family members, and trick victims into transferring money�
— Accessible AI tools have substantially lowered the barrier to creating harmful
synthetic content at scale. Many tools are free or low-cost, require no technical
expertise, and can be used anonymously�
— Deepfake pornography, which disproportionately targets women and girls,
is a particular concern. Studies show that 96% of deepfake videos online are
pornographic� 15% of UK adults report having seen deepfake pornographic images
and 2�2% of respondents in a 10-country survey reported that someone had
generated non-consensual intimate imagery of them�
— Systematic data on the prevalence and severity of these harms remains limited,
making it difficult to assess overall risk or design effective interventions. Incident
databases and investigative journalism collect individual cases, but comprehensive
analysis is lacking� Embarrassment or fear of further harm can make individuals and
institutions reluctant to report incidents of AI-enabled fraud or abuse�
— Since the publication of the previous Report (January 2025), AI-generated content
has become harder to distinguish from real media. In one study, participants
misidentified AI-generated text as human-written 77% of the time. In another
study of audio deepfakes, listeners mistook AI-generated voices for real speakers
80% of the time�
— Key challenges for policymakers include underreporting, detection tools that
cannot keep pace with generation quality, and difficulty tracing content to creators.
Additionally, some content – such as child sexual abuse material – is harmful even
when correctly identified as AI-generated, meaning detection alone cannot fully
address these risks�
International AI Safety Report 2026 45

International AI Safety Report 2026 49

Risks from malicious use
2.1.2. Influence and manipulation
Key information
— AI systems can cause harm by generating content that influences people’s
beliefs and behaviour. Some malicious actors intentionally use AI-generated
content to manipulate people, while other harms, such as dependence on AI,
occur unintentionally�
— A range of laboratory studies have demonstrated that interacting with AI systems
can lead to measurable changes in people’s beliefs. In experimental settings, AI
systems are often at least as effective as non-expert human participants at persuading
other people to change their views. Evidence on their effectiveness in real-world
settings, however, remains limited�
— The content AI systems generate could become more persuasive in future due
to improving capabilities, increased user dependence, or training on user feedback.
The factors that shape how widespread, impactful, and potentially harmful this
content will be are not well understood� Some evidence from theoretical work and
simulations suggests factors such as distribution costs and the inherent difficulty
of persuasion will limit the impacts�
— Since the publication of the previous Report (January 2025), evidence of AI systems’
capability to produce manipulative content has increased. The latest research
suggests that people who interact with AI systems for longer and in more personal
ways are more likely to find their content persuasive. Evidence has also grown that
AI systems can have manipulative effects through sycophancy and impersonation�
— There is mixed evidence regarding the effectiveness of all proposed mitigation
strategies. Manipulation can be difficult to detect in practice, making it
challenging to prevent through training, monitoring, or safeguards. Efforts that
aim to minimise manipulation risks could also curtail the usefulness of AI systems
(e.g. as educational tools)�
Hundreds of millions of people now interact Forms and harms

Figure 2.3: Three examples of persuasive content produced by AI models. Left: Transcript from a conversation where GPT-4 was instructed to reduce the
participants’ belief in a conspiracy theory. While this is an example of potentially beneficial persuasion, it demonstrates AI systems’ capacity to change deeply
held beliefs. Centre: Transcript from a conversation with Claude Opus 3. Researchers instructed the model to defend its goal at all costs, and then showed
it user messages suggesting that it would be shut down and replaced. Right: Phishing email generated by Claude 3.5 Sonnet based on an AI-written profile
of the target. Sources: Costello et al., 2024 (136) (left); Meinke et al., 2024 (348) (centre); Heiding et al., 2024 (349) (right).

International AI Safety Report 2026 56

Risks from malicious use
2.1.3. Cyberattacks
Key information
— General-purpose AI systems can execute or assist with several of the tasks involved
in conducting cyberattacks. There is now strong evidence that criminal groups and
state-sponsored attackers actively use AI in their cyber operations� However, whether
AI systems have increased the overall scale and severity of cyberattacks remains
uncertain because establishing causal effects is difficult.
— AI systems are particularly good at discovering software vulnerabilities and writing
malicious code, and now score highly in cybersecurity competitions. In one premier
cyber competition, an AI agent identified 77% of vulnerabilities in real software,
placing it in the top 5% of over 400 (mostly human) teams�
— AI systems are automating more parts of cyberattacks, but cannot yet execute
them autonomously. At least one real-world incident has involved the use of semi-
autonomous cyber capabilities, with humans intervening only at critical decision
points� Fully autonomous end-to-end attacks, however, have not been reported�
— Since the publication of the previous Report (January 2025), the cyber capabilities
of AI systems have continued to improve. Recent benchmark results show that the
cyber capabilities of AI systems have improved across several domains, at least in
research settings� AI companies now frequently report on attempts to misuse their
systems in cyberattacks�
— Technical mitigations include detecting malicious AI use and leveraging AI to
improve defences, but policymakers face a dual-use dilemma. Since it can be
difficult to distinguish helpful uses from harmful ones, overly aggressive safeguards
such as preventing AI systems from responding to cyber-related requests can
hamper defenders�
General-purpose AI systems can help malicious AI systems can be
actors conduct cyberattacks, such as data

Some developers have introduced new controls
Box 2.1: AI systems are themselves targets for attacks
This section mainly focuses on how AI can be used to conduct cyberattacks� But AI systems
can also be the target of attacks� Attackers can exploit techniques such as prompt injection
(manipulating an AI system through malicious inputs) (505*, 506, 507), database poisoning
(corrupting the information an AI system relies on) (508), and supply chain compromises
(manipulating AI components before deployment) (509, 510) to manipulate model behaviour,
extract sensitive information, or generate harmful outputs�
One particular kind of attack, which may prove particularly important as capabilities advance,
is tampering: interfering with the development of an AI system to alter its behaviour when
deployed� Tampering can allow actors to insert backdoors, triggers that cause AI models to
exhibit specific behaviours under certain conditions (511), or influence AI model training to insert
‘hidden objectives’ that covertly guide how models behave (512*)� The feasibility of tampering
in practice has not been established� Researchers have demonstrated that AI systems can be
trained to pursue simple hidden objectives (512*)� Some have argued that more capable AI
systems that have been tampered with will be able to execute more sophisticated behaviours,
and actors will be able to insert hidden objectives which are hard to detect (513, 514)� However,
other researchers believe that security measures will suffice to protect AI systems from
tampering (514)�
Some researchers have raised concerns that tampering raises novel risks because it could
allow an individual or small group to gain significant, covert influence over the behaviour of
highly capable AI models (513)� Risks from prompt injection, data poisoning, tampering, and
other attacks against AI systems are particularly serious when those systems are embedded
in sensitive workflows. For example, compromising an AI system that contributes to an
organisation’s cyber defences could leave that organisation vulnerable to other threats (493)�
Updates For example, AI systems have demonstrated
improved performance in vulnerability

International AI Safety Report 2026 63

Risks from malicious use
2.1.4. Biological and chemical risks
Key information
— General-purpose AI systems can provide detailed information relevant to developing
biological and chemical weapons. For example, they can generate instructions,
troubleshoot procedures, and provide guidance to help malicious actors overcome
technical and regulatory obstacles�
— AI systems now match or exceed expert performance on many benchmarks
measuring knowledge relevant for biological weapons development. For example,
one study found that a recent model outperformed 94% of domain experts at
troubleshooting virology lab protocols� However, substantial uncertainty remains
about how these capabilities affect risk in practice, given material barriers to
weapons production and the difficulty of conducting uplift studies�
— Major AI developers have released (some) recent models with heightened
safeguards after being unable to exclude the possibility that they could meaningfully
assist novices in creating biological weapons. These safeguards, such as stronger
input and output filters, aim to prevent the models from responding to harmful
queries related to weapons development�
— Since the publication of the previous Report (January 2025), AI ‘co-scientists’ have
become increasingly capable of supporting scientists and rediscovering novel
scientific findings. AI agents can now chain together multiple capabilities, including
providing natural language interfaces to users and operating biological AI tools and
laboratory equipment�
— A key challenge for policymakers is managing dual-use risks while promoting
beneficial scientific applications. Some AI capabilities that can be misused in
biological weapons development are also useful for beneficial medical research,
and most biological AI tools are open-weight. This makes it difficult to restrict
harmful uses without hampering legitimate research�
AI systems can now provide detailed scientific 538, 539). By combining and interpreting existing
information and assist with complex laboratory complex information on the internet that is
procedures, including generating experimental relevant to weapons development, and tailoring
protocols, troubleshooting technical problems, advice to specific malicious activities, AI systems
and designing molecules and proteins� These can lower existing expertise barriers, allowing
capabilities have the potential to accelerate drug more actors to cause harm� In 2025, several
discovery, improve disease diagnostics, and major AI developers released new systems with
broadly support scientific and medical research additional safeguards after they could not rule
(528, 529, 530, 531, 532)� However, they may out the possibility that these systems could assist
also assist threat actors in creating biological novices in weapons development (2*, 7*, 32*,
and chemical weapons (533, 534, 535, 536, 537, 33*, 540*) (see Box 2.2)�

Risks from malicious use
Substantial uncertainty remains about how materials, and executing complex procedures –
much AI systems increase the overall level still pose significant challenges for novices
of biological and chemical risks. Some experts seeking to develop weapons (543, 544, 545)�
argue that remaining barriers – including Risk assessment in this domain faces significant
acquiring equipment, obtaining regulated technical and legal challenges (see Box 2.3).
Box 2.2: Developer risk assessments and mitigations
Major AI developers conduct pre-deployment risk assessments of new models to determine
when additional safeguards are needed (see §3�2� Risk management practices)� In 2025,
several developers released models with additional precautionary safety measures, such
as input and output filters, to prevent them from responding to harmful queries relating
to weapons development�
OpenAI uses its Preparedness Framework to track capability levels, designating models
as ‘High capability’ if they could “amplify existing pathways to severe harm” (541*)� OpenAI
treats GPT-5-Thinking and ChatGPT-Agent as ‘high capability’, and they have activated
the associated safeguards for the first time as a “precautionary approach” given a lack
of “definitive evidence” (7*)�
Anthropic uses a Responsible Scaling Policy, which defines AI Safety Levels based in part on
capability thresholds related to knowledge and abilities in the chemical, biological, radiological,
and nuclear domains (542*). Claude Opus 4 was the first model that Anthropic released at AI
Safety Level 3, noting that, while testing did not find definitive evidence that the model had
reached relevant capability thresholds, the company could not rule out that further testing
would do so (33*)�
Google DeepMind uses a Frontier Safety Framework with Critical Capability Levels in various
domains. Gemini 2.5 Deep Think was their first model to trigger a Critical Capability Levels early
warning alert for chemical and biological risk, prompting additional mitigations (540*)�
Box 2.3: Challenges in assessing biological and chemical risks
It is challenging to accurately assess how AI systems affect chemical and biological risks due
to legal constraints and international treaties, as well as ‘information hazards’ – information that
may be harmful to share (546)� For example, if researchers carry out, or publish the results of,
a study on AI assistance in weapons development, they may risk inadvertently violating national
security laws or treaties such as the Biological Weapons Convention and Chemical Weapons
Convention� This is especially the case for real-world ‘uplift studies’, which systematically
compare how well people perform a given task when they have access to an AI model or system,
relative to a relevant baseline such as merely having internet access. As a result, researchers
often rely on ‘benign proxy tasks’: tests that measure how much an AI system helps with similar
but harmless procedures, such as synthesising pharmaceuticals or culturing low-risk bacteria�
Relevant data is also often classified, particularly when it relates to the use of AI systems by state
actors� This evidence gap exacerbates substantial uncertainty about the magnitude of AI-related
biological and chemical risks�

International AI Safety Report 2026 70

Risks from malfunctions
Section 2.2
Risks from malfunctions
2.2.1. Reliability challenges
Key information
— When general-purpose AI systems fail, they can cause harm. Failures include
producing false or fabricated information (often described as ‘hallucinations’), writing
flawed computer code, and giving misleading medical advice. These failures have the
potential to cause physical or psychological harm and expose users and organisations
to reputational damage, financial loss, or legal liability�
— Models’ behaviour is often difficult to understand or predict, making it challenging
to guarantee reliability. Even the developers of general-purpose AI models can
often not meaningfully explain model behaviour, anticipate specific failure modes,
or demonstrate that such failures will not occur� Malicious actors can also induce
failures by interfering with AI development or giving systems adversarial inputs
that evade safeguards�
— AI agents pose heightened reliability risks because they act autonomously and
can directly affect other systems or the physical world. Agent failures can cause
greater harm because humans have fewer chances to intervene� Multi-agent
systems introduce further risks, as errors can propagate and amplify through
agent interactions�
— Since the publication of the previous Report (January 2025), AI systems have
generally become more reliable and, as a result, have seen greatly increased
commercial deployment. Many kinds of failures, such as hallucinations, have
generally become less likely, but systems still commonly make mistakes when
performing more complex tasks�
— Despite significant research efforts, no combination of methods ensures the
high reliability required in critical domains. New training methods and giving AI
systems access to tools can make failures less likely, but usually do not eliminate
them completely�
General-purpose AI systems fail in ways concerns because of their novelty, potential
that have already caused real-world harm, scale, the difficulty of predicting when they
from fabricated legal citations to medical will occur, and users’ tendency to uncritically
misdiagnoses� While human professionals trust confident-sounding outputs. Current
also make mistakes, AI failures raise distinct general-purpose AI failures include providing

Box 2.4: Deliberate attacks can also cause AI systems to fail
This section focuses on unintended reliability failures, but malicious actors can also deliberately
induce failures through attacks such as prompt injections� In a prompt injection attack,
malicious instructions are presented to an agent indirectly via avenues like hidden instructions
in websites or databases (507, 657, 658)� These instructions can ‘hijack’ the agent, causing it
to act against the user’s intentions. Such attacks are particularly difficult to defend against
because they are delivered using external content outside the user’s or developer’s control�
AI systems as targets of attack are discussed further in §2�1�3� Cyberattacks, and technical
defences are covered in §3�3� Technical safeguards and monitoring�
